In a <a href="/wiki/Chosen-ciphertext_attack" title="Chosen-ciphertext attack">chosen-ciphertext attack</a>, Eve may be able to <i>choose</i> ciphertexts and learn their corresponding plaintexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hac_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-hac-6">[6]</a></sup> Finally in a <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle</a> attack Eve gets in between Alice (the sender) and Bob (the recipient), accesses and modifies the traffic and then forwards it to the recipient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VwMxk_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-VwMxk-55">[55]</a></sup> Also important, often overwhelmingly so, are mistakes (generally in the design or use of one of the <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_protocol" title="Cryptographic protocol">protocols</a> involved).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG"><img alt="Kaiserschloss Kryptologen monument numbers on stele" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1981" data-file-width="2770" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG/220px-2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG/330px-2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG/440px-2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2008-09_Kaiserschloss_Kryptologen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a> monument (<i>center</i>) to Polish cryptanalysts whose breaking of Germany's Enigma machine ciphers, beginning in 1932, altered the course of World War II</div></div></div>
<p>Cryptanalysis of symmetric-key ciphers typically involves looking for attacks against the block ciphers or stream ciphers that are more efficient than any attack that could be against a perfect cipher. For example, a simple brute force attack against DES requires one known plaintext and 2<sup>55</sup> decryptions, trying approximately half of the possible keys, to reach a point at which chances are better than even that the key sought will have been found. But this may not be enough assurance; a <a href="/wiki/Linear_cryptanalysis" title="Linear cryptanalysis">linear cryptanalysis</a> attack against DES requires 2<sup>43</sup> known plaintexts (with their corresponding ciphertexts) and approximately 2<sup>43</sup> DES operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-junod_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-junod-56">[56]</a></sup> This is a considerable improvement over brute force attacks.
</p><p>Public-key algorithms are based on the computational difficulty of various problems. The most famous of these are the difficulty of <a href="/wiki/Integer_factorization" title="Integer factorization">integer factorization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Semiprime" title="Semiprime">semiprimes</a> and the difficulty of calculating <a href="/wiki/Discrete_logarithm" title="Discrete logarithm">discrete logarithms</a>, both of which are not yet proven to be solvable in <a href="/wiki/P_(complexity)" title="P (complexity)">polynomial time</a> (<b>P</b>) using only a classical <a href="/wiki/Turing_completeness" title="Turing completeness">Turing-complete</a> computer. Much public-key cryptanalysis concerns designing algorithms in <b>P</b> that can solve these problems, or using other technologies, such as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_computing" title="Quantum computing">quantum computers</a>. For instance, the best-known algorithms for solving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptic_curve_cryptography" title="Elliptic curve cryptography">elliptic curve-based</a> version of discrete logarithm are much more time-consuming than the best-known algorithms for factoring, at least for problems of more or less equivalent size. Thus, to achieve an equivalent strength of encryption, techniques that depend upon the difficulty of factoring large composite numbers, such as the RSA cryptosystem, require larger keys than elliptic curve techniques. For this reason, public-key cryptosystems based on elliptic curves have become popular since their invention in the mid-1990s.
</p><p>While pure cryptanalysis uses weaknesses in the algorithms themselves, other attacks on cryptosystems are based on actual use of the algorithms in real devices, and are called <i><a href="/wiki/Side-channel_attack" title="Side-channel attack">side-channel attacks</a></i>. If a cryptanalyst has access to, for example, the amount of time the device took to encrypt a number of plaintexts or report an error in a password or PIN character, he may be able to use a <a href="/wiki/Timing_attack" title="Timing attack">timing attack</a> to break a cipher that is otherwise resistant to analysis. An attacker might also study the pattern and length of messages to derive valuable information; this is known as <a href="/wiki/Traffic_analysis" title="Traffic analysis">traffic analysis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWT_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWT-57">[57]</a></sup> and can be quite useful to an alert adversary. Poor administration of a cryptosystem, such as permitting too short keys, will make any system vulnerable, regardless of other virtues. <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(security)" title="Social engineering (security)">Social engineering</a> and other attacks against humans (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bribery" title="Bribery">bribery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extortion" title="Extortion">extortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackmail" title="Blackmail">blackmail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>, ...) are usually employed due to being more cost-effective and feasible to perform in a reasonable amount of time compared to pure cryptanalysis by a high margin.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cryptographic_primitives">Cryptographic primitives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cryptographic primitives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much of the theoretical work in cryptography concerns <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_primitive" title="Cryptographic primitive">cryptographic <i>primitives</i></a>—algorithms with basic cryptographic properties—and their relationship to other cryptographic problems. More complicated cryptographic tools are then built from these basic primitives. These primitives provide fundamental properties, which are used to develop more complex tools called <i>cryptosystems</i> or <i>cryptographic protocols</i>, which guarantee one or more high-level security properties. Note, however, that the distinction between cryptographic <i>primitives</i> and cryptosystems, is quite arbitrary; for example, the RSA algorithm is sometimes considered a cryptosystem, and sometimes a primitive. Typical examples of cryptographic primitives include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudorandom_function" title="Pseudorandom function">pseudorandom functions</a>, <a href="/wiki/One-way_function" title="One-way function">one-way functions</a>, etc.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cryptosystems">Cryptosystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cryptosystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cryptosystems" title="List of cryptosystems">List of cryptosystems</a></div>
<p>One or more cryptographic primitives are often used to develop a more complex algorithm, called a cryptographic system, or <i>cryptosystem</i>. Cryptosystems (e.g., <a href="/wiki/ElGamal_encryption" title="ElGamal encryption">El-Gamal encryption</a>) are designed to provide particular functionality (e.g., public key encryption) while guaranteeing certain security properties (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Chosen-plaintext_attack" title="Chosen-plaintext attack">chosen-plaintext attack (CPA)</a> security in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random_oracle_model" title="Random oracle model">random oracle model</a>). Cryptosystems use the properties of the underlying cryptographic primitives to support the system's security properties. As the distinction between primitives and cryptosystems is somewhat arbitrary, a sophisticated cryptosystem can be derived from a combination of several more primitive cryptosystems. In many cases, the cryptosystem's structure involves back and forth communication among two or more parties in space (e.g., between the sender of a secure message and its receiver) or across time (e.g., cryptographically protected <a href="/wiki/Backup" title="Backup">backup</a> data). Such cryptosystems are sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_protocol" title="Cryptographic protocol">cryptographic protocols</a></i>.
</p><p>Some widely known cryptosystems include RSA, <a href="/wiki/Schnorr_signature" title="Schnorr signature">Schnorr signature</a>, <a href="/wiki/ElGamal_encryption" title="ElGamal encryption">ElGamal encryption</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Good_Privacy" title="Pretty Good Privacy">Pretty Good Privacy</a> (PGP). More complex cryptosystems include <a href="/wiki/Electronic_cash" title="Electronic cash">electronic cash</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hplni_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hplni-58">[58]</a></sup> systems, <a href="/wiki/Signcryption" title="Signcryption">signcryption</a> systems, etc. Some more 'theoretical'<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="What's &quot;theoretical&quot; about it? They are implementable and proven to work, unlike theories which are partially proven conjecture. (December 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> cryptosystems include <a href="/wiki/Interactive_proof_system" title="Interactive proof system">interactive proof systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YSSq3_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-YSSq3-59">[59]</a></sup> (like <a href="/wiki/Zero-knowledge_proof" title="Zero-knowledge proof">zero-knowledge proofs</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7Blaw_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-7Blaw-60">[60]</a></sup> systems for <a href="/wiki/Secret_sharing" title="Secret sharing">secret sharing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrgUy_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-KrgUy-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oedVM_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-oedVM-62">[62]</a></sup> etc.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lightweight_cryptography">Lightweight cryptography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lightweight cryptography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lightweight cryptography (LWC) concerns cryptographic algorithms developed for a strictly constrained environment. The growth of <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">Internet of Things (IoT)</a> has spiked research into the development of lightweight algorithms that are better suited for the environment. An IoT environment requires strict constraints on power consumption, processing power, and security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desUp_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-desUp-63">[63]</a></sup> Algorithms such as <a href="/wiki/PRESENT" title="PRESENT">PRESENT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Encryption_Standard" title="Advanced Encryption Standard">AES</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speck_(cipher)" title="Speck (cipher)">SPECK</a> are examples of the many LWC algorithms that have been developed to achieve the standard set by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VYBze_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-VYBze-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:Applications_of_cryptography" title="Category:Applications of cryptography">Category:Applications of cryptography</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="60" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out  into another article titled <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Applications_of_cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Applications of cryptography (page does not exist)">Applications of cryptography</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Cryptography" title="Talk:Cryptography">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(December 2021)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General">General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cryptography is widely used on the internet to help protect user-data and prevent eavesdropping. To ensure secrecy during transmission, many systems use private key cryptography to protect transmitted information. With public-key systems, one can maintain secrecy without a master key or a large number of keys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> But, some algorithms like <a href="/wiki/BitLocker" title="BitLocker">Bitlocker</a> and <a href="/wiki/VeraCrypt" title="VeraCrypt">Veracrypt</a> are generally not private-public key cryptography. Such as Veracrypt, it uses a password hash to generate the single private key. However, it can be configured to run in public-private key systems. The <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> opensource encryption library <a href="/wiki/OpenSSL" title="OpenSSL">OpenSSL</a> provides <a href="/wiki/Free_and_open-source_software" title="Free and open-source software">free and opensource</a> encryption software and tools. The most commonly used encryption cipher suit is <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Encryption_Standard" title="Advanced Encryption Standard">AES</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> as it has hardware acceleration for all <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> based processors that has <a href="/wiki/AES_instruction_set" title="AES instruction set">AES-NI</a>. A close contender is <a href="/wiki/ChaCha20-Poly1305" title="ChaCha20-Poly1305">ChaCha20-Poly1305</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Stream_cipher" title="Stream cipher">stream cipher</a>, however it is commonly used for mobile devices as they are <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture_family" title="ARM architecture family">ARM</a> based which does not feature AES-NI instruction set extension. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cybersecurity">Cybersecurity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cybersecurity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cryptography can be used to secure communications by encrypting them. Websites use encryption via <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> "End-to-end" encryption, where only sender and receiver can read messages, is implemented for email in <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Good_Privacy" title="Pretty Good Privacy">Pretty Good Privacy</a> and for secure messaging in general in <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Signal_(software)" title="Signal (software)">Signal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telegram_(software)" title="Telegram (software)">Telegram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Operating systems use encryption to keep passwords secret, conceal parts of the system, and ensure that software updates are truly from the system maker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> Instead of storing plaintext passwords, computer systems store hashes thereof; then, when a user logs in, the system passes the given password through a <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_hash_function" title="Cryptographic hash function">cryptographic hash function</a> and compares it to the hashed value on file. In this manner, neither the system nor an attacker has at any point access to the password in plaintext.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Encryption is sometimes used to encrypt one's entire drive. For example, <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a> has implemented <a href="/wiki/BitLocker" title="BitLocker">BitLocker</a> (a program by Microsoft) to render drive data opaque without users logging in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cryptocurrencies_and_cryptoeconomics">Cryptocurrencies and cryptoeconomics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cryptocurrencies and cryptoeconomics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cryptographic techniques enable <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency" title="Cryptocurrency">cryptocurrency</a> technologies, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_Ledger_Technology" title="Distributed Ledger Technology">distributed ledger technologies</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">blockchains</a>), which finance <a href="/wiki/Cryptoeconomics" title="Cryptoeconomics">cryptoeconomics</a> applications such as <a href="/wiki/Decentralized_finance" title="Decentralized finance">decentralized finance (DeFi)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-69">[69]</a></sup> Key cryptographic techniques that enable cryptocurrencies and cryptoeconomics include, but are not limited to: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptographic_keys" title="Cryptographic keys">cryptographic keys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_hash_function" title="Cryptographic hash function">cryptographic hash functions</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_key_encryption" title="Public key encryption">asymmetric (public key) encryption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multi-factor_authentication" title="Multi-factor authentication">Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA)</a>, <a href="/wiki/End-to-end_encryption" title="End-to-end encryption">End-to-End Encryption (E2EE)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zero-knowledge_proof" title="Zero-knowledge proof">Zero Knowledge Proofs (ZKP)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_issues">Legal issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legal issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptography_laws_in_different_nations" title="Cryptography laws in different nations">Cryptography laws in different nations</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibitions">Prohibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Prohibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cryptography has long been of interest to intelligence gathering and <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_agency" title="Law enforcement agency">law enforcement agencies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RangerSteve1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-RangerSteve1-10">[10]</a></sup> Secret communications may be criminal or even <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treasonous</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. Because of its facilitation of <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a>, and the diminution of privacy attendant on its prohibition, cryptography is also of considerable interest to civil rights supporters. Accordingly, there has been a history of controversial legal issues surrounding cryptography, especially since the advent of inexpensive computers has made widespread access to high-quality cryptography possible.
</p><p>In some countries, even the domestic use of cryptography is, or has been, restricted. Until 1999, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> significantly restricted the use of cryptography domestically, though it has since relaxed many of these rules. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Republic_of_Iran" title="Islamic Republic of Iran">Iran</a>, a license is still required to use cryptography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cryptolaw_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-cryptolaw-8">[8]</a></sup> Many countries have tight restrictions on the use of cryptography. Among the more restrictive are laws in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wHfOu_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-wHfOu-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, cryptography is legal for domestic use, but there has been much conflict over legal issues related to cryptography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RangerSteve1_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-RangerSteve1-10">[10]</a></sup> One particularly important issue has been the <a href="/wiki/Export_of_cryptography" title="Export of cryptography">export of cryptography</a> and cryptographic software and hardware. Probably because of the importance of cryptanalysis in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and an expectation that cryptography would continue to be important for national security, many Western governments have, at some point, strictly regulated export of cryptography. After World War II, it was illegal in the US to sell or distribute encryption technology overseas; in fact, encryption was designated as auxiliary military equipment and put on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Munitions_List" title="United States Munitions List">United States Munitions List</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyberlaw_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyberlaw-71">[71]</a></sup> Until the development of the <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a>, asymmetric key algorithms (i.e., public key techniques), and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, this was not especially problematic. However, as the Internet grew and computers became more widely available, high-quality encryption techniques became well known around the globe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Export_controls">Export controls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Export controls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Export_of_cryptography" title="Export of cryptography">Export of cryptography</a></div>
<p>In the 1990s, there were several challenges to US export regulation of cryptography. After the <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_Zimmermann" title="Philip Zimmermann">Philip Zimmermann</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pretty_Good_Privacy" title="Pretty Good Privacy">Pretty Good Privacy</a> (PGP) encryption program found its way onto the Internet in June 1991, a complaint by <a href="/wiki/RSA_Security" title="RSA Security">RSA Security</a> (then called RSA Data Security, Inc.) resulted in a lengthy criminal investigation of Zimmermann by the US Customs Service and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a>, though no charges were ever filed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zim_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-zim-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levybook_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-levybook-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_J._Bernstein" title="Daniel J. Bernstein">Daniel J. Bernstein</a>, then a graduate student at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UC_Berkeley" title="UC Berkeley">UC Berkeley</a>, brought a lawsuit against the US government challenging some aspects of the restrictions based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1st_Amendment" title="1st Amendment">free speech</a> grounds. The 1995 case <i><a href="/wiki/Bernstein_v._United_States" title="Bernstein v. United States">Bernstein v. United States</a></i> ultimately resulted in a 1999 decision that printed source code for cryptographic algorithms and systems was protected as <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">free speech</a> by the United States Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b_v_us_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-b_v_us-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1996, thirty-nine countries signed the <a href="/wiki/Wassenaar_Arrangement" title="Wassenaar Arrangement">Wassenaar Arrangement</a>, an arms control treaty that deals with the export of arms and "dual-use" technologies such as cryptography. The treaty stipulated that the use of cryptography with short key-lengths (56-bit for symmetric encryption, 512-bit for RSA) would no longer be export-controlled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wa_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-wa-75">[75]</a></sup> Cryptography exports from the US became less strictly regulated as a consequence of a major relaxation in 2000;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ftoOu_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ftoOu-76">[76]</a></sup> there are no longer very many restrictions on key sizes in US-<a href="/wiki/Export_of_cryptography" title="Export of cryptography">exported</a> mass-market software. Since this relaxation in US export restrictions, and because most personal computers connected to the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> include US-sourced <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browsers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a> or <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer" title="Internet Explorer">Internet Explorer</a>, almost every Internet user worldwide has potential access to quality cryptography via their browsers (e.g., via <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">Transport Layer Security</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Thunderbird" title="Mozilla Thunderbird">Mozilla Thunderbird</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Outlook" title="Microsoft Outlook">Microsoft Outlook</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-mail_client" title="E-mail client">E-mail client</a> programs similarly can transmit and receive emails via TLS, and can send and receive email encrypted with <a href="/wiki/S/MIME" title="S/MIME">S/MIME</a>. Many Internet users don't realize that their basic application software contains such extensive <a href="/wiki/Cryptosystem" title="Cryptosystem">cryptosystems</a>. These browsers and email programs are so ubiquitous that even governments whose intent is to regulate civilian use of cryptography generally don't find it practical to do much to control distribution or use of cryptography of this quality, so even when such laws are in force, actual enforcement is often effectively impossible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NSA_involvement">NSA involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: NSA involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_Security_Agency_headquarters,_Fort_Meade,_Maryland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2265" data-file-width="2904" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg/220px-National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg/330px-National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg/440px-National_Security_Agency_headquarters%2C_Fort_Meade%2C_Maryland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_Security_Agency_headquarters,_Fort_Meade,_Maryland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>NSA headquarters in Fort Meade, Maryland</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Clipper_chip" title="Clipper chip">Clipper chip</a></div>
<p>Another contentious issue connected to cryptography in the United States is the influence of the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> on cipher development and policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RangerSteve1_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-RangerSteve1-10">[10]</a></sup> The NSA was involved with the design of <a href="/wiki/Data_Encryption_Standard" title="Data Encryption Standard">DES</a> during its development at <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and its consideration by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Standards" title="National Bureau of Standards">National Bureau of Standards</a> as a possible Federal Standard for cryptography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cryptogram_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-cryptogram-77">[77]</a></sup> DES was designed to be resistant to <a href="/wiki/Differential_cryptanalysis" title="Differential cryptanalysis">differential cryptanalysis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coppersmith-des_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-coppersmith-des-78">[78]</a></sup> a powerful and general cryptanalytic technique known to the NSA and IBM, that became publicly known only when it was rediscovered in the late 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kmod9_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kmod9-79">[79]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Steven_Levy" title="Steven Levy">Steven Levy</a>, IBM discovered differential cryptanalysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levybook_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-levybook-73">[73]</a></sup> but kept the technique secret at the NSA's request. The technique became publicly known only when Biham and Shamir re-discovered and announced it some years later. The entire affair illustrates the difficulty of determining what resources and knowledge an attacker might actually have.
</p><p>Another instance of the NSA's involvement was the 1993 <a href="/wiki/Clipper_chip" title="Clipper chip">Clipper chip</a> affair, an encryption microchip intended to be part of the <a href="/wiki/Capstone_(cryptography)" title="Capstone (cryptography)">Capstone</a> cryptography-control initiative. Clipper was widely criticized by cryptographers for two reasons. The cipher algorithm (called <a href="/wiki/Skipjack_(cipher)" title="Skipjack (cipher)">Skipjack</a>) was then classified (declassified in 1998, long after the Clipper initiative lapsed). The classified cipher caused concerns that the NSA had deliberately made the cipher weak in order to assist its intelligence efforts. The whole initiative was also criticized based on its violation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerckhoffs%27s_Principle" title="Kerckhoffs's Principle">Kerckhoffs's Principle</a>, as the scheme included a special <a href="/wiki/Key_escrow" title="Key escrow">escrow key</a> held by the government for use by law enforcement (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Telephone_tapping" title="Telephone tapping">wiretapping</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levybook_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-levybook-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_rights_management">Digital rights management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Digital rights management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Digital_rights_management" title="Digital rights management">Digital rights management</a></div>
<p>Cryptography is central to digital rights management (DRM), a group of techniques for technologically controlling use of <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyrighted</a> material, being widely implemented and deployed at the behest of some copyright holders. In 1998, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._President" title="U.S. President">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Millennium_Copyright_Act" title="Digital Millennium Copyright Act">Digital Millennium Copyright Act</a> (DMCA), which criminalized all production, dissemination, and use of certain cryptanalytic techniques and technology (now known or later discovered); specifically, those that could be used to circumvent DRM technological schemes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMCA_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMCA-80">[80]</a></sup> This had a noticeable impact on the cryptography research community since an argument can be made that any cryptanalytic research violated the DMCA. Similar statutes have since been enacted in several countries and regions, including the implementation in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directive_on_the_harmonisation_of_certain_aspects_of_copyright_and_related_rights_in_the_information_society" title="Directive on the harmonisation of certain aspects of copyright and related rights in the information society">EU Copyright Directive</a>. Similar restrictions are called for by treaties signed by <a href="/wiki/World_Intellectual_Property_Organization" title="World Intellectual Property Organization">World Intellectual Property Organization</a> member-states.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> have not enforced the DMCA as rigorously as had been feared by some, but the law, nonetheless, remains a controversial one. <a href="/wiki/Niels_Ferguson" title="Niels Ferguson">Niels Ferguson</a>, a well-respected cryptography researcher, has publicly stated that he will not release some of his research into an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intel_Corporation" title="Intel Corporation">Intel</a> security design for fear of prosecution under the DMCA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-y0bus_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-y0bus-81">[81]</a></sup> Cryptologist <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Schneier" title="Bruce Schneier">Bruce Schneier</a> has argued that the DMCA encourages <a href="/wiki/Vendor_lock-in" title="Vendor lock-in">vendor lock-in</a>, while inhibiting actual measures toward cyber-security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dOE5r_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-dOE5r-82">[82]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Alan_Cox_(computer_programmer)" title="Alan Cox (computer programmer)">Alan Cox</a> (longtime <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a> developer) and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Felten" title="Edward Felten">Edward Felten</a> (and some of his students at Princeton) have encountered problems related to the Act. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dmitry_Sklyarov" title="Dmitry Sklyarov">Dmitry Sklyarov</a> was arrested during a visit to the US from Russia, and jailed for five months pending trial for alleged violations of the DMCA arising from work he had done in Russia, where the work was legal. In 2007, the cryptographic keys responsible for <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a> and <a href="/wiki/HD_DVD" title="HD DVD">HD DVD</a> content scrambling were <a href="/wiki/AACS_encryption_key_controversy" title="AACS encryption key controversy">discovered and released onto the Internet</a>. In both cases, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MPAA" title="MPAA">Motion Picture Association of America</a> sent out numerous DMCA takedown notices, and there was a massive Internet backlash<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AACS_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-AACS-11">[11]</a></sup> triggered by the perceived impact of such notices on <a href="/wiki/Fair_use" title="Fair use">fair use</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech">free speech</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_disclosure_of_encryption_keys">Forced disclosure of encryption keys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cryptography&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Forced disclosure of encryption keys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Key_disclosure_law" title="Key disclosure law">Key disclosure law</a></div>
<p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_Investigatory_Powers_Act_2000" title="Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act 2000">Regulation of Investigatory Powers Act</a> gives UK police the powers to force suspects to decrypt files or hand over passwords that protect encryption keys. Failure to comply is an offense in its own right, punishable on conviction by a two-year jail sentence or up to five years in cases involving national security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_law_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-UK_law-9">[9]</a></sup> Successful prosecutions have occurred under the Act; the first, in 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vZk6J_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-vZk6J-83">[83]</a></sup> resulted in a term of 13 months' imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8jof6_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-8jof6-84">[84]</a></sup> Similar forced disclosure laws in Australia, Finland, France, and India compel individual suspects under investigation to hand over encryption keys or passwords during a criminal investigation.
</p><p>In the United States, the federal criminal case of <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Fricosu" title="United States v. Fricosu">United States v. Fricosu</a></i> addressed whether a search warrant can compel a person to reveal an <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encryption</a> <a href="/wiki/Passphrase" title="Passphrase">passphrase</a> or password.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9ML0W_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-9ML0W-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> (EFF) argued that this is a violation of the protection from self-incrimination given by the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifth Amendment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vxom2_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vxom2-86">[86]</a></sup> In 2012, the court ruled that under the <a href="/wiki/All_Writs_Act" title="All Writs Act">All Writs Act</a>, the defendant was required to produce an unencrypted hard drive for the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qpsUI_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-qpsUI-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many jurisdictions, the legal status of forced disclosure remains unclear.
</p><p>The 2016 <a href="/wiki/FBI%E2%80%93Apple_encryption_dispute" title="FBI–Apple encryption dispute">FBI–Apple encryption dispute</a> concerns the ability of courts in the United States to compel manufacturers' assistance in unlocking cell phones whose contents are cryptographically protected.
</p><p>As a potential counter-measure to forced disclosure some cryptographic software supports <a href="/wiki/Plausible_deniability" title="Plausible deniability">plausible deniability</a>, where the encrypted data is indistinguishable from unused random data (for example such as that of a <a href="/wiki/Data_remanence" title="Data remanence">drive which has been securely wiped</a>).
</p>

<li id="cite_note-cryptolaw-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-cryptolaw_8-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-cryptolaw_8-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.cryptolaw.org/cls2.htm" rel="nofollow">"Overview per country"</a>. <i>Crypto Law Survey</i>. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Books_on_cryptography" title="Books on cryptography">Books on cryptography</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em">
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFArbibDwyer2011">Arbib, Jonathan; Dwyer, John (31 January 2011). <i>Discrete Mathematics for Cryptography</i> (1 ed.). Algana Publishing. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-907934-01-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-907934-01-8"><bdi>978-1-907934-01-8</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Discrete+Mathematics+for+Cryptography&amp;rft.edition=1&amp;rft.pub=Algana+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2011-01-31&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-907934-01-8&amp;rft.aulast=Arbib&amp;rft.aufirst=Jonathan&amp;rft.au=Dwyer%2C+John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACryptography"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBecket,_B1988">Becket, B (1988). <i>Introduction to Cryptology</i>. Blackwell Scientific Publications. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-632-01836-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-632-01836-9"><bdi>978-0-632-01836-9</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/16832704" rel="nofollow">16832704</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+Cryptology&amp;rft.pub=Blackwell+Scientific+Publications&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F16832704&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-632-01836-9&amp;rft.au=Becket%2C+B&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACryptography"></span> Excellent coverage of many classical ciphers and cryptography concepts and of the "modern" DES and RSA systems.</li>
<li><i>Cryptography and Mathematics</i> by Bernhard Esslinger, 200 pages, part of the free open-source package <a href="/wiki/CrypTool" title="CrypTool">CrypTool</a>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110722183013/http://www.cryptool.org/download/CrypToolScript-en.pdf" rel="nofollow">"PDF download"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Archived from the original on 22 July 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 December</span> 2013</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=PDF+download&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cryptool.org%2Fdownload%2FCrypToolScript-en.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACryptography"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_web" title="Template:Cite web">cite web</a>}}</code>:  CS1 maint: bot: original URL status unknown (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_bot:_original_URL_status_unknown" title="Category:CS1 maint: bot: original URL status unknown">link</a>)</span>. CrypTool is the most widespread e-learning program about cryptography and cryptanalysis, open source.</li>
<li><i>In Code: A Mathematical Journey</i> by <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Flannery" title="Sarah Flannery">Sarah Flannery</a> (with David Flannery). Popular account of Sarah's award-winning project on public-key cryptography, co-written with her father.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Gannon_(author)" title="James Gannon (author)">James Gannon</a>, <i>Stealing Secrets, Telling Lies: How Spies and Codebreakers Helped Shape the Twentieth Century</i>, Washington, D.C., Brassey's, 2001, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-57488-367-4" title="Special:BookSources/1-57488-367-4">1-57488-367-4</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oded_Goldreich" title="Oded Goldreich">Oded Goldreich</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.wisdom.weizmann.ac.il/~oded/foc-book.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Foundations of Cryptography</i></a>, in two volumes, Cambridge University Press, 2001 and 2004.</li>
<li><i>Alvin's Secret Code</i> by <a href="/wiki/Clifford_B._Hicks" title="Clifford B. Hicks">Clifford B. Hicks</a> (children's novel that introduces some basic cryptography and cryptanalysis).</li>
<li><i><a class="external text" href="http://www.cs.umd.edu/~jkatz/imc.html" rel="nofollow">Introduction to Modern Cryptography</a></i> by Jonathan Katz and Yehuda Lindell.</li>
<li>Ibrahim A. Al-Kadi, "The Origins of Cryptology: the Arab Contributions," Cryptologia, vol. 16, no. 2 (April 1992), pp. 97–126.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060709111152/http://www.crypto.rub.de/en_paar.html" rel="nofollow">Christof Paar</a>, Jan Pelzl, <a class="external text" href="http://www.cryptography-textbook.com/" rel="nofollow"><i>Understanding Cryptography, A Textbook for Students and Practitioners</i>.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20201031190651/http://cryptography-textbook.com/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 31 October 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> Springer, 2009. (Slides, online cryptography lectures and other information are available on the companion web site.) Very accessible introduction to practical cryptography for non-mathematicians.</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.mpepil.com" rel="nofollow">"Max Planck Encyclopedia of Public International Law"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Max+Planck+Encyclopedia+of+Public+International+Law&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mpepil.com&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACryptography"></span>, giving an overview of international law issues regarding cryptography.</li>
<li><i>Introduction to Modern Cryptography</i> by <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Rogaway" title="Phillip Rogaway">Phillip Rogaway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mihir_Bellare" title="Mihir Bellare">Mihir Bellare</a>, a mathematical introduction to theoretical cryptography including reduction-based security proofs. <a class="external text" href="http://www.cs.ucdavis.edu/~rogaway/classes/227/spring05/book/main.pdf" rel="nofollow">PDF download</a>.</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFStallings2013"><a href="/wiki/William_Stallings" title="William Stallings">Stallings, William</a> (March 2013). <i>Cryptography and Network Security: Principles and Practice</i> (6th ed.). Prentice Hall. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-13-335469-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-13-335469-0"><bdi>978-0-13-335469-0</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Cryptography+and+Network+Security%3A+Principles+and+Practice&amp;rft.edition=6th&amp;rft.pub=Prentice+Hall&amp;rft.date=2013-03&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-13-335469-0&amp;rft.aulast=Stallings&amp;rft.aufirst=William&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACryptography"></span></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tenzer,_Theo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tenzer, Theo (page does not exist)">Tenzer, Theo</a> (2021): <i>SUPER SECRETO – The Third Epoch of Cryptography: Multiple, exponential, quantum-secure and above all, simple and practical Encryption for Everyone</i>, Norderstedt, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9783755761174" title="Special:BookSources/9783755761174">9783755761174</a>.</li>
<li>Johann-Christoph Woltag, 'Coded Communications (Encryption)' in Rüdiger Wolfrum (ed) <i>Max Planck Encyclopedia of Public International Law</i> (Oxford University Press 2009).</li></ul>
